  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_d0dc8f2d-3f41-4f59-b36f-d5e88a072635'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };

      initAppleId();

      initializeSiwa();

<span id="freeTextContainer6954567497492668979">Presenting by popular demand one of the most anticipated collections! The legendary artist Carl Barks created Scrooge McDuck and chronicled many of his most popular adventures. In addition to superb storytelling and wonderful entertainment, he left behind a character who was not only rich in his stories, but one for whom the stories themselves were rich. Modern master Don </span>
  <span id="freeText6954567497492668979" style="display:none">Presenting by popular demand one of the most anticipated collections! The legendary artist Carl Barks created Scrooge McDuck and chronicled many of his most popular adventures. In addition to superb storytelling and wonderful entertainment, he left behind a character who was not only rich in his stories, but one for whom the stories themselves were rich. Modern master Don Rosa, beginning in 1994, undertook the task of recounting Uncle Scrooge's past in a serialized epic. The wonderful result of his efforts is now collected in trade paperback form by Gemstone Publishing as THE LIFE &amp; TIMES OF SCROOGE McDUCK.A collection of the celebrated 12-part Eisner Award-winning series that details the life of the young Uncle Scrooge. The story was originally serialized in the United States in <i>Uncle Scrooge</i> comics. Now it has been collected in one all-encompassing popularly-priced volume.</span>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_3023518000" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3023518000" />
    <a title="Mario the lone bookwolf (semi reviewing hiatus ) " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/78485297-mario-the-lone-bookwolf-semi-reviewing-hiatus"><img alt="Mario the lone bookwolf (semi reviewing hiatus ) " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588320656p2/78485297.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3023518000?book_show_action=true">Oct 24, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mario the lone bookwolf (semi reviewing hiatus ) " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mario the lone bookwolf (semi reviewing hiatus ) " href="/user/show/78485297-mario-the-lone-bookwolf-semi-reviewing-hiatus">Mario the lone bookwolf (semi reviewing hiatus ) </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/78485297-mario-the-lone-bookwolf-semi-reviewing-hiatus?shelf=comics">comics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3023518000" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1594479285320788966">That is far beyond a comic or graphic novel, as they call them nowadays, this is art, subtility, irony, reflection and wisdom. A masterpiece of one of the greatest comic artists and writers of all time. All the subtle social critic, the innuendos, the background events. The ingenuity to fit both the situational and superficial humor for kids and the deeper, hidden messages for the adults. A mixture of real events and persons with fiction, an appraisal of the industrial age and capitalism, a char</span>
  <span id="freeText1594479285320788966" style="display:none">That is far beyond a comic or graphic novel, as they call them nowadays, this is art, subtility, irony, reflection and wisdom. A masterpiece of one of the greatest comic artists and writers of all time. All the subtle social critic, the innuendos, the background events. The ingenuity to fit both the situational and superficial humor for kids and the deeper, hidden messages for the adults. A mixture of real events and persons with fiction, an appraisal of the industrial age and capitalism, a character reminiscing about himself, ethic questions and the relativity of good and evil. An allegory about the worth and meaning of money, friendship, love and the history of mankind.<br /> <br />I could go on like that, so please just read it! It has nothing to do with average Disney cliches and downplaying of controversial topics, it is pure and distilled soul and brain food. I guess I have read it already 3 to 4 times over the years and still find new aspects in it, like elements of stories hidden in other stories and extreme continuity, coherence and consequence. And, as if it couldn´t get even better, Don Rosa is so modest to say that he finds his work average. Just wow man.<br /><br />Tropes show how literature is made and which mixture of elements makes works and genres unique: <br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://tvtropes.org/pmwiki/pmwiki.php/ComicBook/TheLifeAndTimesOfScroogeMcDuck">https://tvtropes.org/pmwiki/pmwiki.ph...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="1594479285320788966" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_73499723" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/73499723" />
    <a title="Tyas" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1445709-tyas"><img alt="Tyas" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442509243p2/1445709.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/73499723?book_show_action=true">Oct 05, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tyas" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tyas" href="/user/show/1445709-tyas">Tyas</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1445709-tyas?shelf=comic">comic</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer73499723" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1362303791073415567">I grew up reading Disney's comics, and one thing I like about how Disney handles their comic authors - although they may not write down their names or sign their work - is that the authors have the freedom of depicting Disney's characters quite freely. In the case of Uncle Scrooge, there are some authors who portray him as a real evil, greedy capitalist; while some others take another approach, showing Scrooge as a kindhearted duck who doesn't want to show his generosity, afraid of people taking</span>
  <span id="freeText1362303791073415567" style="display:none">I grew up reading Disney's comics, and one thing I like about how Disney handles their comic authors - although they may not write down their names or sign their work - is that the authors have the freedom of depicting Disney's characters quite freely. In the case of Uncle Scrooge, there are some authors who portray him as a real evil, greedy capitalist; while some others take another approach, showing Scrooge as a kindhearted duck who doesn't want to show his generosity, afraid of people taking advantages of him.<br /><br />The authors are also free to write down the kind of story they like best and employ their own style; thus we have dark, psychological stories of Mickey; lighthearted, family-mouse Mickey; private eye Mickey; adventurous Mickey, etc.<br /><br />With this liberty, Eisner Award-winner Don Rosa could create a masterpiece, a work that will always be remembered: an effort not only to construct Uncle Scrooge's past, but also the long history of the McDuck family, leading all the way to Donald and his nephews.<br /><br />Blending history and pure fiction, featuring the likes of Theodore Roosevelt, Geronimo and other real-life characters, this book accompanies us through a journey as a witness to the making of modern America and the rise of capitalism there in the 19th and early 20th century - and how the piles of money don't really make Scrooge really happy in the end. So in the twelfth episode, the closing chapter of the epic, a clever take on <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5326.A_Christmas_Carol__Great_Stories_" title="A Christmas Carol (Great Stories) by Charles Dickens" rel="nofollow noopener">The Christmas Carol</a> (you know, <i>Scrooge</i> and all that...), the last of the McDucks learns once again of how important family is to him.<br /><br />This is an ambitious work, diving deep into the characters of Scrooge and his family, but the results leave you ultra-satisfied yet craving for more. Even Mickey, the icon of the Disney kingdom, doesn't have a family history as complete as this.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1362303791073415567" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer12233405906561934713"><i>"Life is like a hurricane, here in Duckburg. Race cars, lasers, aeroplanes - it's a duck blur!"</i><br/><br/>If you have no idea what I'm talking about then YOU HAVE NOT LIVED!! DuckTales happened to be my THE most favourite show on TV as a kid. I loved it then and I love it now.   <span id="freeText12233405906561934713" style="display:none"><i>"Life is like a hurricane, here in Duckburg. Race cars, lasers, aeroplanes - it's a duck blur!"</i><br /><br />If you have no idea what I'm talking about then YOU HAVE NOT LIVED!! DuckTales happened to be my THE most favourite show on TV as a kid. I loved it then and I love it now. So you can imagine my delight when I found this little treasure.<br /><br />This book focuses more on Scrooge McDuck and gives us a detailed insight into his past by telling the tale of how Scrooge came to be the richest duck in the world (his wealth amounts to five multiplujillion, nine impossibidillion, seven fantasticatrillion dollars and sixteen cents)<br /><br />It's beautifully illustrated and a complete laugh-riot. Keep an eye out for Scrooge's sister, Hortense - Donald's mum - later on in the book. You can definitely see the family resemblance there! You'll run into many familiar characters like Donald and the triplets, Glomgold, Goldie, Magica De Spell and even the Beagle Boys(or their ancestors, at least)!. The book leaves you begging for the good ol' 90's again.<br /><br />A MUST-READ for any hardcore DuckTales fan!</span>

  <b>tougher than the toughies, and sharper than the sharpies</b>
</i>, is the best duck of them all. Don Rosa tells the epic story of his adventurous life, and manages to create a determined, honest (most of the time), well-crafted character, who can in many ways be an inspiration. Try hard and fail, and then try harder and fail again and again, but never give up, until you are the richest man in the world. Nah, just kidding. But Scrooge’s journey is indeed a reminder that we should belie</span>
  <span id="freeText18052072464605334792" style="display:none">Scrooge McDuck, <i>
  <b>tougher than the toughies, and sharper than the sharpies</b>
</i>, is the best duck of them all. Don Rosa tells the epic story of his adventurous life, and manages to create a determined, honest (most of the time), well-crafted character, who can in many ways be an inspiration. Try hard and fail, and then try harder and fail again and again, but never give up, until you are the richest man in the world. Nah, just kidding. But Scrooge’s journey is indeed a reminder that we should believe in our dreams and chase them, even when they seem impossible to catch. Sometimes funny, sometimes heartbreaking, Don Rosa’s take on the life of this iconic duck, has a lot more to offer than just a few laughs.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1514659894i/24800520._SX540_.jpg" width="428" height="299" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>

<div id="review_51969155" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/51969155" />
    <a title="Brent" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1332955-brent"><img alt="Brent" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1459725095p2/1332955.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/51969155?book_show_action=true">Apr 08, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brent" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brent" href="/user/show/1332955-brent">Brent</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">anyone</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1332955-brent?shelf=carl-barks">carl-barks</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer51969155" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1434630674060122325"> As kids my brother and I collected comic books. One day we found a HUGE box of old comics at a swap meet, which we promptly convinced our parents to buy. When we got home we divided up our loot Jeffy (my brother) got the Spiderman, X-men,and Fantastic Four, while I got the Batman, Ironman and Daredevil, and we divided up the rest of the superheros Jeffy took most of the Marvel stuff because we liked them the best(thats the way older brothers are) and I got the D.C. ultimatly we were both happy.</span>
  <span id="freeText1434630674060122325" style="display:none"> As kids my brother and I collected comic books. One day we found a HUGE box of old comics at a swap meet, which we promptly convinced our parents to buy. When we got home we divided up our loot Jeffy (my brother) got the Spiderman, X-men,and Fantastic Four, while I got the Batman, Ironman and Daredevil, and we divided up the rest of the superheros Jeffy took most of the Marvel stuff because we liked them the best(thats the way older brothers are) and I got the D.C. ultimatly we were both happy. There was however still a large pile of leftover stuff that neither one us wanted like Richie Rich, Archie Comics, Disney, and all the different war comics you could ever want. Unable to just let them go I took them and stuck them in the back of my collection. Well monthes passed and I read all my superhero stuff and my brothers (despite his protest over my tresapassing into his collection) and suddenly it was over I had gone through the aforethought endless supply of funny books, it was devastating in some small way. Unable to cope with my loss I reluctantly made my way to that box with all the "junk" in it. I began with the war comics Sgt. Rock, The Unknown Soldier, The Losers, Blackhawk, etc. they were at least books about war. Turns out they were quite good (but thats another review for another day) but all too soon I had gone through them all. I pressed on. Archie Comics were next and again I was pleasantly surprised. Next was Ritchie Rich, Casper The Friendly Ghost, etc. these were O.K., better than I expected but I knew I had reached the bottem of the barrel. All that was left to quell my appetite was the Disney Comics. Ah the Disney Comics they were at the bottem of my bottem pile of comics and on the bottem of the Disney pile lay Uncle Scrooge I had never even heard of Uncle Scrooge. But alas like so many times in life theese unwanted, unread, books turned out to be some of the best stuff I have ever read. To this day I've found few others as good. Of course they were written by Carl Barks an underapperciated genius famous only in comic book lore, but a genius none the less.<br /> The Life and Times of Scrooge McDuck was not written by Carl Barks, but by a fellow who went to great GREAT lengths to pay homage to the legend that was Carl Barks (Kino Don Rosa). I think he did a fantastic job.<br /> I would recomend Uncle Scrooge to anyone, and being married to a teacher makes me realize what an important tool they could be for anyone who is trying to make history come alive and be relevant for a younger audience.<br /> I usally try to keep theese reviews short (I am a big believer in brevity) but I guess I got a little nostalgic here, I apologize.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1434630674060122325" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1027852932" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1027852932" />
    <a title="Ethan Long" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19618126-ethan-long"><img alt="Ethan Long" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442885086p2/19618126.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1027852932?book_show_action=true">Aug 15, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ethan Long" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ethan Long" href="/user/show/19618126-ethan-long">Ethan Long</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1027852932" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10738727697364854730">So incredibly good!<br/><br/>I've always liked Scrooge McDuck, mostly because of Duck Tales. So when I saw he had his own biography comic books, I was all for it. The thing I wasn't expecting was for them to be really, REALLY good!<br/><br/>Scrooge wasn't always rich, in fact he was really poor. And he worked for everything he got. The first few stories are awesome as they travel through riverboating to cowboys in the West to prospecting across the world. The interesting thing about the stories is that Scrooge actu</span>
  <span id="freeText10738727697364854730" style="display:none">So incredibly good!<br /><br />I've always liked Scrooge McDuck, mostly because of Duck Tales. So when I saw he had his own biography comic books, I was all for it. The thing I wasn't expecting was for them to be really, REALLY good!<br /><br />Scrooge wasn't always rich, in fact he was really poor. And he worked for everything he got. The first few stories are awesome as they travel through riverboating to cowboys in the West to prospecting across the world. The interesting thing about the stories is that Scrooge actually fails! A lot! I think that's what endeared him to me so much was that he not only built an empire on hard work, but after multiple attempts. Granted, all the backstabbing and betrayal throughout the years from the people around him hardened him to the scroogey Scrooge his is, but we all know how that ended up when his triplet nephews showed up. I was just impressed with the storytelling, it doesn't seem like the kind of books that would be that quality, but it really is.<br /><br />And the art is pretty fantastic. Don Rosa puts an insane amount of detail in his books. There's the main story going on on every page, but there are at least 3 or more gags going on in the background per page as well. You have to really look or you'll miss half the jokes! And any wide scene deserves scrutiny, because all those little people are each doing unique things. Good stuff.<br /><br />I would own this book in a second, but because of its age and apparent rarity, I can't shell out the $100 or more that vendors want for it. That's a shame because it's a quality read.<br /><br />If you can find it (I read it from the library, only one in the network had it and it was almost destroyed), read it. You'll be surprised how good it is!</span>
  <a data-text-id="10738727697364854730" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_999819119" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/999819119" />
    <a title="Phandom Duck" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/33164839-phandom-duck"><img alt="Phandom Duck" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1422316272p2/33164839.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/999819119?book_show_action=true">Jul 18, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Phandom Duck" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Phandom Duck" href="/user/show/33164839-phandom-duck">Phandom Duck</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer999819119" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17241317174853586279">Scrooge McDuck, the grumpy old businessman, who possesess more wealth than anyone could ever imagine and who happens to be Donald Duck's uncle. That's all, right?<br/><br/>Of course not! And we have to thank Carl Barks -to begin with- and Don Rosa for adding more to the shallow statement above.<br/><br/>The little poor boy from Scotland, who started off from polishing shoes and earned his first dime from a local worker (well, not quite from a local worker, as we learn in one of the lost episodes, but I'm hearing th</span>
  <span id="freeText17241317174853586279" style="display:none">Scrooge McDuck, the grumpy old businessman, who possesess more wealth than anyone could ever imagine and who happens to be Donald Duck's uncle. That's all, right?<br /><br />Of course not! And we have to thank Carl Barks -to begin with- and Don Rosa for adding more to the shallow statement above.<br /><br />The little poor boy from Scotland, who started off from polishing shoes and earned his first dime from a local worker (well, not quite from a local worker, as we learn in one of the lost episodes, but I'm hearing the "spoiler alert" bells ringing, so I'm dropping this part), went on to become the richest duck in the world. However, as we learn by reading Don Rosa's masterpiece, it's not the money that makes Scrooge one of the richest heroes in the history of comics. It's the tremendous amount of experience he gained through the places he visited, the people he met and the decisions he had to make. <br /><br />He became the "Terror of Transvaal" and the "King of Klondike", he got to befriend Theodore "Teddy" Roosevelt himself and, while still young and poor, he chose to respect a local cult in Australia over becoming rich in no time, by stealing an artefact no one would notice was missing, until it was way too late.<br /><br />Ultimately, Scrooge is not so complex a character. He craves money as much as he craves adventure, he enjoys working hard to succeed and he holds family in the highest esteem, even though he often seems to get angry at his nephew's, Donald, laziness, and threatens to disinherit him, if he doesn't start taking life more seriously.<br /><br />But how he came to this point? Which events shaped him into the rigid yet pure-hearted man (er... duck, I mean) he is today? Which are the memories he cherishes and allows to drown him in an ocean of sweet nostalgia?<br /><br />The answers to all these questions lie within the pages of this epic comic book. Based on Carl Barks' hilarious and exciting tales and infiltrated with Don Rosa's remarkable narrative and drawing talent, the "Life And Times Of Scrooge McDuck" sheds light to the story of one of the most beloved paper heroes of all time. And it's totally worth every single second spent to read it as thoroughly as possible.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17241317174853586279" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_153900655" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/153900655" />
    <a title="Curtis Hempler" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4326067-curtis-hempler"><img alt="Curtis Hempler" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1287284024p2/4326067.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/153900655?book_show_action=true">Mar 12, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Curtis Hempler" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Curtis Hempler" href="/user/show/4326067-curtis-hempler">Curtis Hempler</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4326067-curtis-hempler?shelf=comics">comics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4326067-curtis-hempler?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4326067-curtis-hempler?shelf=childrens-books">childrens-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer153900655" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7420011749432694141">I read this with my 5 year old son, and it has been a perfect before-bed book. Each chapter is a stand-alone story, but there is an over-arching story of how Scrooge McDuck traveled the world and made his fortune.<br/><br/>There is a great Indiana Jones style sense of adventure and exploring the unknown, without anything too scary or disturbing. The only exception is when Scrooge cheats the chief of a village, and ends up with a zombie that will follow him to the ends of the earth. This is a bit creepy if</span>
  <span id="freeText7420011749432694141" style="display:none">I read this with my 5 year old son, and it has been a perfect before-bed book. Each chapter is a stand-alone story, but there is an over-arching story of how Scrooge McDuck traveled the world and made his fortune.<br /><br />There is a great Indiana Jones style sense of adventure and exploring the unknown, without anything too scary or disturbing. The only exception is when Scrooge cheats the chief of a village, and ends up with a zombie that will follow him to the ends of the earth. This is a bit creepy if you have a kid sensitive to that kind of thing, but it also shows Scrooge paying a dire consequence the one time he chose to not `play fair`. The stories aren`t heavy with `morals`, but the few moral lessons that Rosa includes work well with the story. <br /><br />The art is gorgeous and colorful. There are always sight-gags and jokes happening in the background, which my son got a huge kick out of (especially when he noticed things that I missed, and he got to point out the jokes to me).<br /><br />The only problem with this book is it caused a lot of arguments when it was time to close it up and go to sleep.<br /><br />I`m glad I picked up the hard cover volumes, because I think this one will stay on my son`s shelf for many years to come.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7420011749432694141" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2205775371" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer6122510571209885561">I have of course read them before, but actually never in this collection. And not actually this collection either, I read the Swedish version but couldn't find it.<br/><br/>Scrooge McDuck is my absolute favourite character, and Don Rosa is seriously the only "duckist" I like. I remember reading Kalle o Co when I was a kid and was always drawn to Rosa's darker illustrations, with all the shading and funny things going on in the background. Of course I didn't know what a master Don Rosa was until I read the</span>
  <span id="freeText6122510571209885561" style="display:none">I have of course read them before, but actually never in this collection. And not actually this collection either, I read the Swedish version but couldn't find it.<br /><br />Scrooge McDuck is my absolute favourite character, and Don Rosa is seriously the only "duckist" I like. I remember reading Kalle o Co when I was a kid and was always drawn to Rosa's darker illustrations, with all the shading and funny things going on in the background. Of course I didn't know what a master Don Rosa was until I read the collected works in the "Hall of Fame" editions. All that fact checking, historical correctness, staying true both to actual history and Barks' history is really amazing. <br /><br />I enjoyed the "Hall of Fame" a little more, can't really say why right now but this is still truly the "Great American Novel" of all times. Would like to read it in English though since the best, most Scroogian quote - when Scrooge becomes the richest in the world and Matilda calls him "a poor old man" - becomes sort of meaningless in Swedish. </span>

<div id="review_744077441" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/744077441" />
    <a title="Ioan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11463451-ioan"><img alt="Ioan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1343805779p2/11463451.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/744077441?book_show_action=true">Oct 18, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ioan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ioan" href="/user/show/11463451-ioan">Ioan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11463451-ioan?shelf=comics">comics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11463451-ioan?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer744077441&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer744077441" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1883039541076044765">Although willful to get my hands on any fresh FB release containing Carl Barks' Duck stories that I adored in my childhood, I am sort of ashamed to admit that I hadn't had any major interest in Don Rosa's stories, until I stumbled over the so-called "Grande Epopee de Picsou". Yeps, that's the French version of Life and Times of Scrooge McDuck. Took a few really good looks, got really excited, started browsing for this to order it.<br/>Tough luck - I got pretty late, and nowadays if you have a really </span>
  <span id="freeText1883039541076044765" style="display:none">Although willful to get my hands on any fresh FB release containing Carl Barks' Duck stories that I adored in my childhood, I am sort of ashamed to admit that I hadn't had any major interest in Don Rosa's stories, until I stumbled over the so-called "Grande Epopee de Picsou". Yeps, that's the French version of Life and Times of Scrooge McDuck. Took a few really good looks, got really excited, started browsing for this to order it.<br />Tough luck - I got pretty late, and nowadays if you have a really strong heart, like me, you might see prices such as 1,700$ raised for the original English version on the Amazon, and though cardiac arrest won't catch you in its grip, you'll probably have your eyes rolling like I had. Neither the Like News and other used types are particularly cheap, they get well around 100$. So I started thinking and re-thinking whether I should get the French version for 20 something euros. Being an English addict, I had big hesitations, but finally I concluded that it was Rosa's art and the characters that interested me most.<br />And, boy, did I make a good choice. Now I have this thingy in French, but due to a satisfying translation, I have no problems in understanding the text. What about the content itself, the original core? Simply stated: this is pure CREAM. With capital letters as big as ole Scroogey's money bin. Dona's art is expressive, graceful, funny and everything good that Carl Barks' art was, with a touch of Elderesque background gagologic wizardry, albeit more restrained. Couldn't find any flaws even if I tried, and I certainly got so immersed in the Duck universe that I wouldn't do that at all.<br />Aside from the brilliant visuals, the narrations and characterizations are remarkably sturdy. This is no cheap timekiller, its grandeur reminds me strongly of what Hemingway said - you don't need grand words to convey grand feelings. Each story got me reading avidly, each page requests several minutes for its full bouquet to take over the senses, and the ending of the book is one of the most revealing and beautiful endings possible. It summarizes the essence of Scrooge McDuck's apparent worship of money - it is not a love of money that burns inside Scrooge, but a love of all the things that his past provided him with, each cent being a tribute that he paid to those who raised him and to his own capacity of going the right way, most often not the easy one. After a youth full of adventure, challenges and gain, Scrooge chooses to keep away from the rest of the world and rejects even those of his kin, yet despite the ridiculously high standards that he imposed to himself and therefore made him so allergic to the others, he cherishes the memories that all that cold money holds. He might be alienated, but he will never forget. A bittersweet ending that left me almost sobbing.<br /><br />If you are no big spender, you might consider buying the cheaper French version while still available. It makes French so much more worth studying. But if you want the English version, you'll probably have to spare some thick dough and then slam the piggy bank really hard. Still, I cannot imagine someone regretting such a buy. One of the great classics of comics of all times, and Barks' shadow shines all over the place. </span>
  <a data-text-id="1883039541076044765" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_9303802" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/9303802" />
    <a title="Jeremy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/106700-jeremy"><img alt="Jeremy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1347575947p2/106700.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/9303802?book_show_action=true">Nov 19, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeremy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeremy" href="/user/show/106700-jeremy">Jeremy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/106700-jeremy?shelf=comics-n-graphnovs">comics-n-graphnovs</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/106700-jeremy?shelf=borrowed-from-someone">borrowed-from-someone</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer9303802" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11586312983580863616">Really interesting to be reading this and John Updike's "Rabbit, Run" at the same time. Both are steeped in rich detail that is by turns hilarious and apt. Both are page-turners of the highest order. Most significant of all, both are beautifully rendered portraits of characters that are as compelling as they are unlikable. <br/><br/>Bound to ideals that make them unknowable, to peers if not readers, both Scrooge and Rabbit are drawn to a life of selfish wallowing, more figuratively in the case of the form</span>
  <span id="freeText11586312983580863616" style="display:none">Really interesting to be reading this and John Updike's "Rabbit, Run" at the same time. Both are steeped in rich detail that is by turns hilarious and apt. Both are page-turners of the highest order. Most significant of all, both are beautifully rendered portraits of characters that are as compelling as they are unlikable. <br /><br />Bound to ideals that make them unknowable, to peers if not readers, both Scrooge and Rabbit are drawn to a life of selfish wallowing, more figuratively in the case of the former, a character most often remembered for treating his vast vaults as swimming pools. Rabbit, like most any young man at heart, wants ultimate freedom and universal acceptance, to be able to give in to his basest whims while maintaining a respectable family life. <br /><br />Both are tenacious, and both court controversy in their own way. Scrooge handily bests anyone (including, but not limited to, bears, giant zombies, and Teddy Roosevelt) stupid enough to impede his rampage of accumulation for its own sake, and Rabbit's natural cockiness, which served him well as a high school basketball superstar, can cut most anyone down to size, regardless of the nature of their intentions. Having become wary of shysters and hangers-on, Scrooge gradually begins to both alienate and take advantage of his once impoverished family, while Rabbit shocks and shames family and friends alike by ditching his pregnant wife and young son and shacking up with a supposed prostitute. <br /><br />Each seek a perfection that no one in their life can approve or comprehend. They are both seemingly incapable of not being themselves, for better or worse. You might not want to make friends for life with either Uncle Scrooge or Harry Angstrom, but once you make their acquaintance they'll weigh heavily on your mind, particularly when you're faced with the consequences of your actions. <br /><br />I doubt many people have ever chanced to begin and end these books alongside one another, but this type of literary happenstance is one of those possibilities that keep me coming back to these book things. An unexpected, and in both cases excellent, pairing. </span>
  <a data-text-id="11586312983580863616" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_546064423" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/546064423" />
    <a title="Orion Tippens" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8321066-orion-tippens"><img alt="Orion Tippens" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1361666378p2/8321066.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/546064423?book_show_action=true">Feb 25, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Orion Tippens" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Orion Tippens" href="/user/show/8321066-orion-tippens">Orion Tippens</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer546064423" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7152862065292971027">The Life and Times of Scrooge McDuck - the collected comic book series that I have relieved and enjoyed again and again after a reread every few years. Also, of my favorite recommendations to anyone that really appreciates comic books and brilliant storytelling.<br/><br/>Also, a comic that chokes me up at times.<br/><br/>This is the story of Scrooge McDuck, from his humble beginnings earning his first dime, to his near ending..coming to the final conclusion of his long, interesting life. In between, are his own mul</span>
  <span id="freeText7152862065292971027" style="display:none">The Life and Times of Scrooge McDuck - the collected comic book series that I have relieved and enjoyed again and again after a reread every few years. Also, of my favorite recommendations to anyone that really appreciates comic books and brilliant storytelling.<br /><br />Also, a comic that chokes me up at times.<br /><br />This is the story of Scrooge McDuck, from his humble beginnings earning his first dime, to his near ending..coming to the final conclusion of his long, interesting life. In between, are his own multiple trials and tribulations..dealing with dishonest scoundrels, family separation, romantic entanglement, succumbing to his personal darkness. There is much humor in between, and moments of epic badassery (dont fuck with Scrooge McDuck!), and some surprises (we find where his nephew Donald probably got his fiery temper from and stubbornness from). The book is revealing and emotionally invoking. <br /><br />Don Rosa is amazing, and the top of his game here as one of my favorite storytellers. His art and writing feels very alive with incredible detail, emotion, color. Enjoying his work means to fall into his rich world of historical relativity, fantastic travel adventures, and talking ducks. He puts a lot of care and detail into his work, much of it derived from decades of Duck comics made great by Carl Barks (as explained in additional notes and an awesome family tree). This particular work is his masterpiece.<br /><br />This book is getting harder to find and not currently cheap. The recent Boom! hardcover reprints (in two parts) are out of print and commanding high prices on the resale market (Don Rosa was anger over lack of royalties and severed ties with the company). The older Gladstone/Gemstone complete edition is even harder to find (company out of business, royalty problems with Disney as well). Still, very worthwhile and a must for every comic book connoisseur. </span>
  <a data-text-id="7152862065292971027" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

  <span id="freeText1791099746156357245" style="display:none">With the new Ducktales show imminent, I thought I'd finally read this. Outside of a few small instances, it has aged pretty well. I can see why it's considered one of those comics that frequently ends up on those "best graphic novels ever" kinda lists. It has a lot of episodic adventures mixed in with an ongoing story. Based on the notes from the author after each chapter, apparently it also has a lot of callbacks to older Scrooge comics as well, but since I haven't read them I can't really comment except to say it's cool that Don Rosa went to the trouble of keeping that continuity while simultaneously telling a riveting "origin story" of sorts for such a well-liked character.</span>

<div id="review_704583936" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/704583936" />
    <a title="Adam Graham" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4468170-adam-graham"><img alt="Adam Graham" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442501458p2/4468170.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/704583936?book_show_action=true">Aug 26, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Adam Graham" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Adam Graham" href="/user/show/4468170-adam-graham">Adam Graham</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer704583936" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1433393605275312426">Kids of my generation grew up watching Ducktales. Though it was a kid's show, the prime attraction had to be Scrooge McDuck, the most dauntless adventurer to ever appear in kid's television. Most of us kids didn't know that Scrooge had a history in comics dating 40 years prior to Duck Tales. We also had no way of knowing how huge the Duck family was in Europe. We did know that Scrooge's back story seemed to be colored with an almost impossible number of adventures.<br/><br/>Many of these adventures came f</span>
  <span id="freeText1433393605275312426" style="display:none">Kids of my generation grew up watching Ducktales. Though it was a kid's show, the prime attraction had to be Scrooge McDuck, the most dauntless adventurer to ever appear in kid's television. Most of us kids didn't know that Scrooge had a history in comics dating 40 years prior to Duck Tales. We also had no way of knowing how huge the Duck family was in Europe. We did know that Scrooge's back story seemed to be colored with an almost impossible number of adventures.<br /><br />Many of these adventures came from the pen of the late Carl Barks. Dan Rosa sets down to write adventures that connect Scrooge's story into a neat narrative, inspired by Bark's tale.<br /><br />The series appeared over a two year period in the 1990s and takes Scrooge from a young poor lad in Scotland to the richest duck in the world. The book features many of Scrooge's wildest adventures and real life characters such as Teddy Roosevelt, Wyatt Earp, and the James boys show up for their cameos along with long-time Scrooge baddies, the Beagle Boys and we get a peak at the Duck family tree.<br /><br />Rosa does a superb job throughout. I like how he is able to help Scrooge's character develop realistically. The changes in Scrooge from the start of the book, to win he makes his first Billion are very believable. The book also does a good job trumpeting two important messages.<br /><br />The first 2/3 of the book emphasizes the importance of hard work and going for your dreams, never giving up or giving in no matter how great the pressure we face. At times, Scrooge's pursuit of self-fulfillment and his dreams are almost like something of Ayn Rand. Yet, we're reminded, greed can be very dangerous, and we make a foolish mistake when we sacrifice people for things. These two messages can be hard to have in the same book, but Rosa weaves them in seemlessly.<br /><br />In the end, what Rosa has written is a marvelous tale that's truly worthy of its larger than life star. It's a great introduction into the world of Duck comics and a worthwhile read for anyone who wants to learn more about Scrooge McDuck.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1433393605275312426" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {

  }

After serving a stint at the Walt Disney studios as an in-betweener and a gag-man, Barks began draw<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText8891712773138546392\" style=\"display:none\">Carl Barks Donald Duck and Uncle Scrooge comics are considered among the greatest artistic and storytelling achievements in the history of the medium. After serving a stint at the Walt Disney studios as an in-betweener and a gag-man, Barks began drawing the comic book adventures of Donald Duck in 1942. He quickly mastered every aspect of cartooning and over the next nearly 30 years created some of the most memorable comics ever drawn as well as some of the most memorable characters: Barks introduced Uncle Scrooge, the charmed and insufferable Gladstone Gander, the daffy inventor Gyro Gearloose, the bumbling and heedless Beagle Boys, the Junior Woodchucks, and many others. Barks alternated between longish, sprawling 20- or 30-page adventure yarns filled with the romance of danger, courage, and derring-do, whose exotic locales spanned the globe, and shorter stories that usually revolved around crazily ingenious domestic squabbles between Donald and various members of the Duckburg cast. Barks s duck stories, famously enjoyed equally by both children and adults, are both evanescent celebrations of courage and perseverance and depictions of less commendable traits greed, resentment, and one-upmanship. Our initial volume begins when Barks had reached his peak 1948-1950. Highlights include: The title story, Lost in the Andes (Barks s own favorite). Donald and the nephews embark on an expedition to Peru to find where square eggs come from only to meet danger in a mysterious valley whose inhabitants all speak with a southern drawl, and where Huey, Dewey, and Louie save Unca Donald s life by learning how to blow square bubbles! Two stories co-starring the unbearably lucky Gladstone, including the epic Race to the South Seas, as Donald and Gladstone try to win Uncle Scrooge s favor by being the first to rescue him from a desert island. Two Christmas stories, including The Golden Christmas Tree, one of Barks s most fantastic stories that pits him and the nephews against a witch who wants to destroy all the Christmas trees in the world. In other stories, Donald plays a TV quiz show contestant and ends up encased in a giant barrel of &quot;Shaky-Jell,&quot; a truant officer who matches wits with his nephews, and a ranch hand who outwits cattle rustlers. These new editions feature meticulously restored and re-colored pages in a beautifully designed, affordable format geared to the mainstream book buyer. The crowning accomplishment of Finland\'s first literary genius, Seven Brothers remains &quot;the greatest Finnish novel of all time&quot;, the classic among the classics in Finnish literature. Published in 1870, in the author\'s 36th year and two years before his untimely death, Seven Brothers laid the foundation for what Kai Laitinen later called &quot;The Great Tradition in Finnish Prose&quot;. This tradition is characterized by realism, humor, respect for the common people, and depiction of nature as both friend and foe. Received at the time of publication by uncomprehending arbiters of literary taste, who still delighted in romantic approaches to literature, Seven Brothers fared poorly in early reviews. Posterity, however, has resurrected the reputation of Aleksis Kivi, and critics, scholars, and readers at large continue to praise the virtues of this trail-blazing, exceedingly rich novel.<\/span>\n <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer3675243759278829460">Keno Don Hugo Rosa (born June 29, 1951), is an American comic book writer and illustrator known best for his stories about Scrooge McDuck, Donald Duck and other Duck Family characters. Many of his stories are built on characters and locations created by Carl Barks. <br/><br/>In 1995 he won the Eisner Award for "Best Serialized Story" for his 12-chapter work The Life and Times of Scrooge McDuck. He received </span>
  <span id="freeText3675243759278829460" style="display:none">Keno Don Hugo Rosa (born June 29, 1951), is an American comic book writer and illustrator known best for his stories about Scrooge McDuck, Donald Duck and other Duck Family characters. Many of his stories are built on characters and locations created by Carl Barks. <br /><br />In 1995 he won the Eisner Award for "Best Serialized Story" for his 12-chapter work The Life and Times of Scrooge McDuck. He received the award again in 1997 for "Best Artist/Writer - Humor Category".</span>
  <a data-text-id="3675243759278829460" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
